Definition of a Prophet and the Types of Divine Inspiration
Sawi defines the term nabi and enumerates the four types of wahi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a prophet (nabi) as a free person who receives divine inspiration (wahi) of rulings, and the four distinct types of wahi.

Core Idea
A prophet is a free person inspired with rulings; inspiration comes through an angel, directly, in the heart, or in a dream.

This passage lays out the basic definition of a prophet, specifying that a prophet must be a free person who receives divine guidance in the form of rulings. The author then clarifies the nature of this inspiration by listing its four types. Understanding this foundation is crucial before exploring the relationship between prophets and messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice the conditions: free, inspired with rulings.
The definition includes a note about the linguistic origin of the word nabi.
2
Identify the four types of wahi
Find the four distinct types of inspiration listed: through an angel, direct conversation, inspiration in the heart, and in a dream.
3
Reflect on the inclusiveness
Ask yourself why the author includes this classification. It shows that wahi is not limited to angelic communication.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حُر أوحي إليه بشرع - أي : أحكام – سواء أُمر بتبليغها ؛ أي : إيصالها للمكلَّفين أم لا ، فإن أُمر بذلك فرسول أيضاً، فالنَّبيُّ أعم من الرسول. لا، وأصله : نبيء بالهمزة كما يدلُّ عليه رواية قراءته بالهمز في التّشهد، فقُلبت الهمزة ياء، وما كانت نبيّاً قط أنثى ولا عبد وشخص ذو افتعال قوله : (أوحي) الوحي : هو الإرسال من الله لعبده بالأحكام وهو أقسام، فيكون تارةً بواسطة ملك ؛ كجبريل، وتارة بمكالمة من الله تعالى من غير واسطة؛ كما وقع لموسى، وتارةً بإلهام يقع في القلب، وتارةً في المنام قوله : ( فالنَّبيُّ أعمُّ من (الرَّسول) : أي : فيلزم من كونه رسولاً أن يكون نبيّاً ولا عكس، ولا يلزم أن يكون له كتاب وهذا هو المشهور، وقيل: النَّبِيُّ والرَّسول مترادفان وقيل : الرَّسول من كان له شرع جديد وكتاب. فإن قلت: قوله تعالى : اللَّهُ يَصْطَفِي مِنَ الْمَلَبِكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِن النَّاسِ (١) : : يفيد أنَّ الرُّسل يكونون من الملائكة أيضاً، وهو خلاف التّعريف . أجيب بأنَّ الرَّسول المعرَّف هنا هو الذي يبلغ الأمم، وأما رسل : الملائكة؛ فهم لتبليغ بعضهم بعضاً، ولتبليغ رسل البشر، فالموضوع مختلف.
A free [person] who is divinely inspired with a law - i.e., rulings - whether or not he is commanded to convey it, i.e., to deliver it to the obligated persons. If he is commanded with that, then he is also a messenger. So the prophet is more general than the messenger. No, and its root is nabi' with a hamzah, as indicated by the recitation reading it with hamzah in the tashahhud. Then the hamzah was changed to a ya', and there never was a female prophet nor a slave, and a person of [؟] ... His statement: (inspired) - inspiration is the sending from God to His servant of rulings, and it has types. It is sometimes through an angel like Jibril, sometimes through a conversation from God without an intermediary as occurred with Musa, sometimes through an inspiration that occurs in the heart, and sometimes in a dream. His statement: (so the prophet is more general than the messenger): i.e., it follows from being a messenger that he is a prophet, but not the reverse, and it does not necessitate that he has a book. This is the well-known view. And it is said: the prophet and messenger are synonymous. And it is said: the messenger is he who has a new law and book. If you say: His statement Almighty: Allah selects from the angels messengers and from people... indicates that messengers are also from angels, which contradicts the definition. It is answered: the messenger defined here is the one who conveys to nations. As for the messengers of the angels, they are for conveying among themselves and for conveying to human messengers, so the subject is different.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'prophet' (nabi) and 'messenger' (rasul) and clarifies their relationship. It begins by defining a prophet as a free person who receives divine inspiration (wahi) of rulings, whether or not he is commanded to convey them. If he is commanded to convey, he is also a messenger. Thus the prophet is more general: every messenger is a prophet but not every prophet is a messenger. The author then notes the linguistic derivation: the word nabi originally had a hamzah (nabi'), as seen in some readings of the tashahhud, and the hamzah was later changed to ya. He also notes that no woman or slave has ever been a prophet. The passage then elaborates on the types of wahi: through an angel (like Jibril), through direct conversation (like Musa), through inspiration in the heart, and through dreams. Regarding the distinction between nabi and rasul, the author presents three views: (1) the well-known view that they differ, with nabi being more general; (2) they are synonymous; (3) a messenger is one who brings a new law and book. An objection is raised: the Quranic verse 'Allah selects from the angels messengers and from people' indicates that messengers can be angels, which contradicts the definition that a messenger is a human prophet who is commanded to convey. The answer is that the definition here applies to human messengers who convey to nations, while angelic messengers are for conveying among themselves or to human messengers, so the subject matter differs. Thus the definition remains consistent.

وحي
إلهام
منام
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage shows that wahi also occurs through direct conversation, inner inspiration, and dreams.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT listed as a type of wahi?
A) Through an angel
B) Through direct conversation
C) Through reading books
D) Through inspiration in the heart

Key Takeaway
A prophet is a free person who receives divine rulings through one of four modes of inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that revelation can come through dreams. Think of a dream that had a profound impact on you. Did you consider it as a possible form of guidance from Allah? If not, why? Will you start paying more attention to your dreams as a potential channel of divine communication?
?
The passage states that no woman or slave ever became a prophet. Does this challenge your understanding of Allah's justice? How do you reconcile this with modern notions of equality? Are you willing to accept that Allah's wisdom may differ from human expectations, even when it makes you uncomfortable?
?
You are not a prophet, but you have the Quran, which is the final and most complete revelation. Do you approach the Quran as direct guidance from Allah to you personally? When was the last time you read a verse and felt it was speaking directly to your situation? What one change will you make today to treat the Quran as living guidance rather than a historical text?

The Relationship Between Prophets and Messengers
Sawi explains the relationship between nabi and rasul and presents three scholarly views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the well-known view that a prophet is more general than a messenger, along with the alternative views on this distinction.

Core Idea
Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

After defining a prophet, the author elaborates on the crucial relationship between prophets and messengers. He states the well-known position that the term 'prophet' is more comprehensive: a messenger is a prophet who is commanded to convey the revelation, but a prophet may not be commanded to convey. This lesson also presents two alternative scholarly views on this matter, offering a complete picture of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the main statement
The passage begins by restating that the prophet is more general than the messenger. Read this carefully.
This is the well-known view.
2
Understand the logical implication
Note that being a messenger implies being a prophet, but the reverse is not true. A prophet does not necessarily have a book.
3
Examine the three views
The passage mentions three opinions: the well-known view (distinction), synonymity, and the view that a messenger brings a new law. Identify each.
These are all reported views in the tradition.

Answering the Objection: Angels as Messengers
Sawi raises and answers an objection about angels being messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the apparent conflict between the definition of a messenger as a human prophet and the Quranic verse mentioning messengers from among the angels.

Core Idea
Angels are messengers in a different sense; they convey among themselves and to human messengers.

The author anticipates an objection based on the Quranic verse 'Allah selects from the angels messengers and from people.' If a messenger is defined as a human prophet commanded to convey, how can angels be considered messengers? The author's answer clarifies that the term 'messenger' is used in different contexts: human messengers convey to nations, while angelic messengers convey within their own ranks or to human prophets. Thus the definition remains consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection cites the Quranic verse that mentions messengers from the angels, which seems to contradict the definition.
The verse is a key proof text.
2
Study the answer
The answer distinguishes between human messengers (defined earlier) and angelic messengers, who have a different function.
Focus on how the author resolves the conflict.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction is important for maintaining a consistent definition.

Linguistic Definitions of 'Nabi' from 'Naba'
Al-Sawi traces the linguistic origin of 'nabi' from 'naba' and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivations of the word 'nabi' (prophet) from the root 'naba' and the objection raised against the active participle meaning, along with its resolution.

Core Idea
'Nabi' carries multiple linguistic layers—informed, informer, or elevated—showing the richness of its meaning.

This lesson examines the linguistic definition of 'prophet' (nabi) as traced by Al-Sawi. He presents three possible derivations from the root 'naba' (news), and then addresses an objection concerning the active participle interpretation. Understanding this clarifies why the term encompasses both receiving and conveying divine news.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full English translation to grasp the author's flow.
The passage includes three derivations and an objection-answer.
2
Identify the three derivations
Find the three proposed origins: passive participle (informed), active participle (informer), and from 'nabwa' (elevation). Note how each is defined.
The first two are from 'naba', the third from 'nabwa'.
3
Work through the objection and answer
Notice the objection: if a prophet is not a messenger, the active meaning fails. Then see the answer: he must still inform people of his prophethood to be respected.
This resolves the apparent conflict.

Definition of prophethood. From 'al-naba' - which is news – in the sense of the passive participle as indicated by the preceding definition; i.e., that God Most High has informed him of rulings. And it is possible that it is in the sense of the active participle; i.e., that he is an informer from God Most High. And it is possible that its origin is 'nabiyu' from 'al-nabwa' i.e., elevation; the waw was changed to ya for the reason mentioned, and the ya was assimilated into it, meaning that he is elevated in rank, or an elevator of it. His saying: (From al-naba and it is news): i.e., it is the linguistic meaning, and upon this the linguistic meaning of 'nabi' is 'the informer'. 'Informed to him'. His saying: (In the sense of the passive participle): i.e., 'nabi' meaning 'munabba' – with fatha on the ba – i.e., His saying: (As indicated by the preceding definition): i.e., where it was said in it: ('he was inspired') His saying: (In the sense of the active participle): i.e., 'nabi'' meaning 'munbi' – with kasra on the ba – i.e., informer; because he is commanded to convey and inform. If you say: if he is not a messenger, then he is not commanded to inform; so the designation does not appear then. The answer is that he is commanded to inform people that he is a prophet so that he may be respected.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic derivation of the word 'nabi' (prophet). The author (Al-Sawi) begins by stating the definition of prophethood, then traces it to three possible roots. First, from 'naba' (news), which yields two interpretations: either the passive participle meaning 'informed by God' (as the earlier definition implies), or the active participle meaning 'informer from God'. The commentary explains each possibility: 'nabi' as 'munabba' (informed) or 'munbi' (informer). An objection is raised: if a prophet is not a messenger, he is not commanded to inform, so the active meaning fails. The answer is that even if not a messenger, he must inform people that he is a prophet to receive respect. This clarifies that the active meaning is valid for all prophets regardless of messenger status.

Key Terms
النبوة
النَّبأ
المخبر
المنبأ
المنبئ

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think 'nabi' only means 'informed' (passive) and that the active meaning only applies to messengers.
Correction: The passage shows that the active meaning (informer) is valid for all prophets, whether messengers or not, because they must announce their prophethood to be respected.

Checkpoint

D) The word 'nabi' cannot be derived from 'naba' as an active participle.

Key Takeaway
The word 'nabi' has multiple valid linguistic roots, each highlighting a different aspect of prophethood, and the active sense is defended even for non-messenger prophets.

Linguistic Definition of Prophet from 'Nabwa'
Al-Sawi explains the derivation of 'nabi' from 'nabwa' meaning elevation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of the word 'nabi' when derived from 'nabwa' (elevation) and why both the active and passive interpretations are valid.

Core Idea
The word 'prophet' can mean both 'elevated' and 'elevator' — two sides of the same linguistic coin.

Al-Sawi continues his linguistic analysis of the term 'nabi'. After considering the derivation from 'naba' (news), he now explores the alternative root 'nabwa' (elevation). This derivation yields two equally valid meanings, grounded in Arabic morphology and analogous to earlier discussions. Understanding this dual sense enriches our appreciation of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Al-Sawi's explanation of the derivation from 'nabwa'.
2
Identify the two meanings
Notice that Al-Sawi interprets 'nabi' as either 'al-murtafi' (the elevated one) or 'al-rafi' (the elevator).
3
Connect to previous discussion
Understand the reference to the declension of 'al-ali' and how it parallels the current derivation.
4
Consider the conclusion
Appreciate that both passive and active senses are valid, as stated in the final line of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (من النَّبوَة) : أي : فمعنى النَّبيِّ لغة : المرتفع أو الرَّافع. :قوله : (لما مرَّ ) أي في تصريف العلي، وما قيل هناك يقال هنا. قوله أو مرتفعها أي قامت به الرِّفعة والأظهر أن يقول كما قال
فهو بمعنى المفعول أو الفاعل أيضاً.

His saying 'or its elevator' meaning elevation is established in him, and the more apparent is that he says as he said. So it is in the sense of the passive participle or the active participle as well.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage explains the second linguistic origin of 'nabi': from 'nabwa' meaning elevation. The author interprets it as either 'al-murtafi' (the elevated one) in the passive sense, or 'al-rafi' (the elevator) in the active sense. He references a previous discussion on the declension of 'al-ali' for analogy. The phrase 'or its elevator' is explained as meaning that elevation (rif'a) is established in him (passive) or he causes it (active). The conclusion is that both interpretations (passive and active) apply, just as with the derivation from 'naba'.

The Meaning of 'al-Mustafa'
Al-Sawi defines 'al-Mustafa' linguistically with hadith support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning and scriptural basis of the title 'al-Mustafa' for the Prophet Muhammad.

Core Idea
'Al-Mustafa' means 'the Chosen One' – a title rooted in divine selection.

Before examining theological arguments about prophecy, we must clarify the titles given to the Prophet. One key title is 'al-Mustafa', which carries a precise morphological meaning and is supported by an authentic hadith. This lesson unpacks that definition and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the direct meaning.
Notice the morphological rule mentioned in the second sentence.
2
Identify the linguistic definition
Find how 'al-Mustafa' is derived from 'al-istifa' (selection). Understand that it is a passive participle meaning 'the chosen one'.
The term is not just a name; it is a descriptive title.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Read the hadith that traces the chain of divine selection from Isma'il to the Prophet himself. Reflect on how this supports the meaning of 'chosen one'.
The hadith emphasizes the Prophet's elevated status as the best of the best.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why it matters that the Prophet is called 'al-Mustafa'. How does this title relate to his role and mission?
This title underscores the Prophet's unique role in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(المصطفى): اسم مفعول من الاصطفاء، وهو الاختيار، فمعناه: المختار .
قوله : (المصطفى) : أصله المصتفى ؛ بتاء مثناة فوقية بعد الصَّاد، قلبت طاء للقاعدة المشهورة.
قوله: (فمعناه المختار) : أي : لما في الحديث الصحيح : «إنَّ الله اصطفى كنانة من ولد إسماعيل، واصطفى قريشاً من كنانة، واصطفى من قريش بني هاشم، واصطفاني من بني هاشم، فأنا خيار من خيار من خيار
('al-Mustafa'): A passive participle from 'al-istifa'', which is selection, so its meaning is 'the chosen one'. His saying: ('al-Mustafa'): Its origin is 'al-mustafa' with a taa marbouta above after the sad, the taa was changed to taa for the well-known rule. His saying: (So its meaning is 'the chosen one'): i.e., because of the authentic hadith: 'Indeed, God chose Kinana from the descendants of Isma'il, and chose Quraysh from Kinana, and chose from Quraysh the Banu Hashim, and chose me from Banu Hashim, so I am the best of the best of the best.'

Commentary
This passage defines the title 'al-Mustafa' applied to the Prophet. It states that it is a passive participle from 'istifa' (selection), meaning 'the chosen one'. The commentary then gives the morphological rule that the original 'al-mustafaa' (with ta) changed to 'al-mustafa' (with ta). It supports the meaning by citing a hadith that recounts the chain of divine selection culminating in the Prophet himself. The hadith shows that the Prophet is the best of the best, emphasizing his chosen status.

The Many Meanings of 'al-Karim' (The Generous)
Al-Sawi defines 'al-Karim' with multiple interpretations and clarifies the most suitable one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'al-Karim' applied to the Prophet encompasses both theological categories (attribute of essence/action) and a specific connotation of noble lineage, with the latter being the most suitable here.

Core Idea
'Al-Karim' is more than generosity—it signifies pure origin for the Prophet.

The title 'al-Karim' is often translated simply as 'The Generous,' but in Islamic theology and when applied to the Prophet Muhammad, it carries deeper dimensions. This passage from al-Sawi unpacks multiple interpretations, distinguishing between attributes of essence and action, and finally highlighting the meaning of noble lineage as the most appropriate for the Prophet. Let's explore these layers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get an overview of the definitions discussed.
Notice the parenthetical phrases – they indicate the base text being commented on.
2
Identify the two theological categories
The passage presents two interpretations: one making 'al-Karim' an attribute of essence (صفة ذات) and the other an attribute of action (صفة فعل). Understand the difference.
Attribute of essence is intrinsic; attribute of action relates to acts.
3
Focus on the 'tayyib' meaning
See why 'at-tayyib' (good/pure) is considered more appropriate here, connecting it to the Prophet's noble lineage.
This meaning is linked to comprehensive attributes.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that 'Karim' only means generous. How does this passage broaden that view?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الكريم) من الكرم، وهو صفة تقتضي الإعطاء لا في نظير شيء، أو هو نفس الإعطاء المذكور ، وقد يراد بالكريم الطَّيِّب، وهو الأنسب هنا ؛ أي: فهو طيب الأصل
غيره : فهو مرفوع الرتبة، أو رافع لرتبة منِ اتَّبعه، فهو بمعنى المفعول أو الفاعل، لفّ ونشر مرتب.
قوله : (وهو صفة تقتضي الإعطاء) : أي : فيكون صفة ذات .
وقوله : (أو هو نفس الإعطاء) : أي فيكون صفة فعل.
قوله : (وهو الأنسب :(هنا :أي لكونه من الصفات الجامعة.
قوله : (طيب الأصل) : أي : النَّسب
('al-Karim') from 'al-karam', and it is an attribute that necessitates giving not in exchange for anything, or it is the giving itself mentioned. And sometimes 'al-Karim' is intended to mean 'at-tayyib' (the good/pure), and that is more appropriate here; i.e., he is of good origin. Other than that: he is elevated in rank, or an elevator of the rank of whoever follows him, so it is in the sense of the passive participle or the active participle, an ordered wrapping and spreading. His saying: (And it is an attribute that necessitates giving): i.e., it is an attribute of essence. And his saying: (Or it is the giving itself): i.e., it is an attribute of action. His saying: (And it is more appropriate here): i.e., because it is among the comprehensive attributes. His saying: (Good origin): i.e., lineage.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the title 'al-Karim' (The Generous) applied to the Prophet. It begins by explaining the root 'karam': either an attribute of essence that necessitates giving without compensation, or the act of giving itself (attribute of action). However, it notes that here 'al-Karim' is more appropriately understood as 'at-tayyib' (the good/pure), meaning good lineage. Then it adds that aside from that meaning, the Prophet is elevated in rank or elevates his followers, again reflecting passive/active interpretations. The commentary elaborates: the first interpretation (giving without compensation) makes it an attribute of essence; the second (the giving itself) makes it an attribute of action. The appropriateness of the 'tayyib' meaning is because the Prophet possesses comprehensive attributes. Finally, 'good origin' is specified as lineage.

Misconception: Believing that 'al-Karim' only means generous (giving freely) without consideration of noble lineage.
Correction: While generosity is one meaning, in this context for the Prophet, 'al-Karim' primarily signifies 'at-tayyib' (good/pure origin), i.e., noble lineage. The passage explicitly states this is the most appropriate meaning.

Apposition of 'Wa Hadhihi Aqidah' with 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Analyzes the apposition of nominal sentences and the condition of declarative nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical condition for apposing a nominal sentence to another, and the nuance when one sentence is performative (insha'iyyah).

Core Idea
Apposition requires matching in declarative nature; performative can be treated as declarative in wording.

This lesson unpacks a subtle grammatical point in the opening phrase 'wa hadhihi aqidah'. The author explains how this phrase relates to the preceding 'al-hamdu lillah' as an apposition, and the conditions for such an apposition to be valid, especially when the first sentence might be considered performative rather than declarative.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on apposition
Read the first part of the source which discusses the apposition. Notice the key terms 'عطف', 'خبرية', 'إنشائية'.
Pay attention to the conditional clauses.
2
Identify the condition for valid apposition
The author states that apposition is appropriate if both sentences are declarative in wording and meaning. Then discusses the case when the first sentence is performative.
Consider the logical implication: if 'al-hamdu lillah' is a performative act of praising, can it be apposed to a declarative statement?
3
Reflect on the solution
The author says that even if one is performative, apposition is permissible if we consider the declarative nature, at least in wording. This shows the flexibility of grammatical analysis.
The phrase 'فتدبر' invites deeper reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( عطف على جملة الحمد لله ) : عطف اسميَّة على مثلها، وهو مناسب إن كان كلٌّ منهما خبريّاً لفظاً ومعنى، وأما على جعل جملة الحمد إنشائية؛ فلا يجوز إلَّا أن يراعى الخبرية ولو باعتبار اللفظ، فتدبر .
قوله : (واسم الإشارة عائد ... إلخ) : هذا أحد احتمالات سبعة مشهورة هو المختار منها ، ثمَّ إن قلنا : إنَّ الذَّهن يقوم به المفصل؛ فالأمر ظاهر، وإن قلنا : إنَّه لا يقوم به المفصَّل ؛ فالكلام على حذف مضاف واحد؛ أي: مفصل هذه إن قلنا : إنَّ أسماء الكتب من قبيل علم الشَّخص، وإن قلنا : إنَّها من قبيل علم الجنس؛ فالكلام على حذف مضافين؛ أي: مفصل نوع هذه .
والحقُّ : أنَّ الذَّهن يقوم به المفصَّل وأسماء الكتب والعلوم من قبيل علم الشَّخص، بناء على أنَّ الشَّيء لا يتعدد بتعدد محله، والفرق تحكم فلا حاجة لتقدير شيء أصلاً.
His statement: '(apposition to the sentence al-hamdu lillah)' – it is an apposition of a nominal sentence to another nominal sentence, and that is appropriate if each of them is declarative in wording and meaning. But if the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' is taken as performative (insha'iyyah), then it is not permissible unless the declarative nature is considered, even if only in wording, so reflect. His statement: 'And the demonstrative pronoun refers back... etc.' – this is one of seven well-known possibilities, and it is the chosen one among them. Then if we say: The mind comprehends the detailed (content), then the matter is clear. And if we say: It does not comprehend the detailed (content), then the text involves the deletion of one genitive complement, i.e., 'the detailed of this' – if we say that names of books are of the type of proper names. And if we say that they are of the type of generic names, then the text involves the deletion of two genitive complements, i.e., 'the detailed of the type of this'. And the truth is: The mind does comprehend the detailed (content), and names of books and sciences are of the type of proper names, based on the principle that a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its loci, and the difference is arbitrary. So there is no need to assume any (deletion) at all.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical analysis of the phrase 'وهذه عقيدة' (and this is a creed) in the main text. It addresses two main points: the nature of the apposition and the reference of the demonstrative pronoun. First, the author explains that the phrase is appositive to the sentence 'الحمد لله' (praise be to Allah). If both are declarative, the apposition is fine. But if 'الحمد لله' is considered performative (e.g., praising), then apposition is only permissible if we regard it as declarative in wording. This is a subtle point about the grammatical status of the basmala/hamdalah in Arabic grammar. Second, the author discusses the demonstrative pronoun 'هذه' (this). He states that there are seven possible interpretations for its referent, and the chosen one is that it refers to something the mind already knows. If the mind can grasp the detailed content (the book's topics), then it is straightforward. If not, then we must assume an omitted word: either 'مفصل هذه' (the detailed of this) if book names are considered proper names, or 'مفصل نوع هذه' (the detailed of the type of this) if they are generic. The author's preferred view is that book names are proper names, and the mind does grasp the detailed content, so no omission is needed. This reflects a principle in logic/grammar: a thing does not multiply by having multiple instances.

Key Terms
عطف
جملة اسمية
خبرية
إنشائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is apposition of 'wa hadhihi aqidah' to 'al-hamdu lillah' not permissible?
A) When both are nominal sentences
B) When 'al-hamdu lillah' is taken as performative and we do not consider its declarative nature
C) When 'wa hadhihi aqidah' is not a complete sentence
D) When the author says it is not permissible

Key Takeaway
Apposition between a performative and declarative sentence is possible if the performative sentence is treated as declarative at least in wording.

Reference of the Demonstrative Pronoun in 'Wa Hadhihi Aqidah'
Analyzes the reference of the demonstrative pronoun and the possible omissions based on the nature of book names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various possibilities for the reference of the demonstrative pronoun 'hadhihi' and the author's preferred interpretation involving the mind's comprehension of detailed content.

Core Idea
The demonstrative pronoun refers to something the mind comprehends, and book names are proper names, so no omitted word is needed.

This lesson examines the second part of the grammatical analysis: the demonstrative pronoun 'hadhihi' in 'wa hadhihi aqidah'. The author presents seven possible interpretations, selects one, and explains the implications for understanding the relationship between the pronoun and its referent. He also discusses the nature of book names and whether they are proper or generic nouns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the demonstrative pronoun
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قوله : (واسم الإشارة عائد ... إلخ)'. Note the mention of seven possibilities and the chosen one.
The author mentions 'المختار' (the chosen one).
2
Understand the two scenarios
The author discusses two scenarios based on whether the mind comprehends the detailed content (المفصل) or not. This leads to different interpretations involving omitted words.
'المفصل' refers to the detailed topics of the book.
3
Grasp the author's preferred view
The author concludes that the mind does comprehend the detailed content, and names of books are proper names, so no omission is needed. This is based on the principle that a thing does not multiply by its loci.
This resolves the need for omitting words like 'مفصل' or 'نوع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( عطف على جملة الحمد لله ) : عطف اسميَّة على مثلها، وهو مناسب إن كان كلٌّ منهما خبريّاً لفظاً ومعنى، وأما على جعل جملة الحمد إنشائية؛ فلا يجوز إلَّا أن يراعى الخبرية ولو باعتبار اللفظ، فتدبر .
قوله : (واسم الإشارة عائد ... إلخ) : هذا أحد احتمالات سبعة مشهورة هو المختار منها ، ثمَّ إن قلنا : إنَّ الذَّهن يقوم به المفصل؛ فالأمر ظاهر، وإن قلنا : إنَّه لا يقوم به المفصَّل ؛ فالكلام على حذف مضاف واحد؛ أي: مفصل هذه إن قلنا : إنَّ أسماء الكتب من قبيل علم الشَّخص، وإن قلنا : إنَّها من قبيل علم الجنس؛ فالكلام على حذف مضافين؛ أي: مفصل نوع هذه .
والحقُّ : أنَّ الذَّهن يقوم به المفصَّل وأسماء الكتب والعلوم من قبيل علم الشَّخص، بناء على أنَّ الشَّيء لا يتعدد بتعدد محله، والفرق تحكم فلا حاجة لتقدير شيء أصلاً.
His statement: '(apposition to the sentence al-hamdu lillah)' – it is an apposition of a nominal sentence to another nominal sentence, and that is appropriate if each of them is declarative in wording and meaning. But if the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' is taken as performative (insha'iyyah), then it is not permissible unless the declarative nature is considered, even if only in wording, so reflect. His statement: 'And the demonstrative pronoun refers back... etc.' – this is one of seven well-known possibilities, and it is the chosen one among them. Then if we say: The mind comprehends the detailed (content), then the matter is clear. And if we say: It does not comprehend the detailed (content), then the text involves the deletion of one genitive complement, i.e., 'the detailed of this' – if we say that names of books are of the type of proper names. And if we say that they are of the type of generic names, then the text involves the deletion of two genitive complements, i.e., 'the detailed of the type of this'. And the truth is: The mind does comprehend the detailed (content), and names of books and sciences are of the type of proper names, based on the principle that a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its loci, and the difference is arbitrary. So there is no need to assume any (deletion) at all.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical analysis of the phrase 'وهذه عقيدة' (and this is a creed) in the main text. It addresses two main points: the nature of the apposition and the reference of the demonstrative pronoun. First, the author explains that the phrase is appositive to the sentence 'الحمد لله' (praise be to Allah). If both are declarative, the apposition is fine. But if 'الحمد لله' is considered performative (e.g., praising), then apposition is only permissible if we regard it as declarative in wording. This is a subtle point about the grammatical status of the basmala/hamdalah in Arabic grammar. Second, the author discusses the demonstrative pronoun 'هذه' (this). He states that there are seven possible interpretations for its referent, and the chosen one is that it refers to something the mind already knows. If the mind can grasp the detailed content (the book's topics), then it is straightforward. If not, then we must assume an omitted word: either 'مفصل هذه' (the detailed of this) if book names are considered proper names, or 'مفصل نوع هذه' (the detailed of the type of this) if they are generic. The author's preferred view is that book names are proper names, and the mind does grasp the detailed content, so no omission is needed. This reflects a principle in logic/grammar: a thing does not multiply by having multiple instances.

Key Terms
اسم الإشارة
المفصل
علم الشخص
علم الجنس
تقدير مضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the demonstrative pronoun directly refers to the word 'عقيدة' without any further analysis.
Correction: The author shows that the reference is more nuanced, involving the detailed content of the book, and requires analysis of whether the mind comprehends it.

Checkpoint
What is the author's final conclusion about the need to assume an omitted word in the phrase 'wa hadhihi aqidah'?
A) One word must be omitted: 'مفصل'
B) Two words must be omitted: 'مفصل نوع'
C) No omission is needed
D) The pronoun refers to 'الحمد لله'

Key Takeaway
The demonstrative pronoun 'hadhihi' refers to the detailed content of the book, which the mind can comprehend, and book names are proper names, so no omitted words are required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'my aqidah,' do you truly know the detailed content it contains, or have you settled for a vague label? What specific beliefs have you not yet examined in detail, and what will you do this week to learn them precisely?
?
The principle that a thing does not become multiple by its loci challenges you to consider your own consistency. In which areas of your life—home, work, online—do you present a different version of your faith? Will you commit to being the same believer in every place, starting today?
?
This lesson shows that the mind can and must grasp the detailed content. Are you feeding your mind with the details of your religion, or are you content with summaries and shortcuts? What is one detailed topic from Islamic creed you will study deeply this month, and how will you hold yourself accountable to that goal?

The Meaning of 'Latif' and Why the Poem is Called al-Kharida
Commentator explains the meaning of 'latif' and why the poem is called al-Kharida.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple meanings of the divine attribute 'Latif' and why the poem is called al-Kharida.

Core Idea
Latif combines kindness, subtle knowledge, and clarity—all reflected in the poem's name.

This passage unlocks a seemingly small word with deep layers. The commentator first grounds us in the linguistic root of 'latif', then shows how it illuminates both a divine attribute and the very nature of the poem we are studying. Understanding this will help you appreciate the richness of Islamic theological terminology.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the linguistic meaning
Read the Arabic and translation for the verb 'latufa'. Notice it means to be good and bestow favor.
The verb pattern is like 'nasara', not 'hasuna'.
2
Apply it to Allah
See how the same root becomes a divine attribute: Allah is the Benefactor. Reflect on why this is 'apparent'.
3
Spot the two interpretations
The commentator gives two ways scholars understood 'al-Latif' – one focuses on hidden knowledge, the other on kindness. Both are rooted in the same word.
This shows the breadth of the attribute.
4
Understand the poem's description
Finally, learn why the poem itself is called 'latif'. It refers to its concise, smooth, or clear style. All are valid.
This connects the attribute to the text you're studying.

وأمَّا (لَطَف) كـ(نصر) فمعناه أحسنَ وأنعم، ومعناه في حقه تعالى ظاهر؛ أي: المحسن المنعم على عباده . وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر اللطيف بالعالم بخفيَّات الأمور، ووجة من فسَّره بالبَرِّ المحسن لعباده . والمراد هنا أنَّها قليلة الألفاظ أو سَلِسَةُ الألفاظ أو واضحتها، والكلُّ صحيح.
And as for (لَطَف) like (نَصَر), its meaning is 'he was good and bestowed favor,' and its meaning in relation to Him, exalted, is apparent; that is: the Benefactor, the Bestower of favor upon His servants. And by this you know the basis of the one who interpreted 'al-Latif' as the Knower of subtle matters, and the basis of the one who interpreted it as the Kindly Benefactor to His servants. And what is intended here is that it (the poem) is few in words, or smooth in wording, or clear in wording, and all are correct.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the word 'latif' as used in the title of the poem 'al-Kharidat'. The commentator begins by stating the linguistic meaning of the verb 'latufa' (to be kind/bountiful), then applies it to God as the Benefactor. He then shows how different scholars interpreted the divine attribute 'al-Latif': some as the Knower of hidden things (subtleties), others as the Kindly Benefactor. Finally, he clarifies that the poem is called 'latif' because it is concise in words, or smooth in style, or clear—all valid interpretations. The passage follows the author's own order: first the linguistic and theological meaning, then the application to the poem, ending with the affirmation that all interpretations are correct.

Small Poem, Great Knowledge: Understanding Its Content
Commentator exalts the poem's knowledge and outlines its contents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poem is considered great in knowledge despite its small size, and you will identify the key theological topics it addresses.

Core Idea
Size does not measure worth: the poem packs the core of Islamic theology.

The commentator acknowledges that the poem's small size might lead one to underestimate its value. He clarifies that its greatness lies not in length but in the profound meanings and definitive proofs it contains. This passage sets the stage for the entire work by highlighting its essential content.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator first mentions the poem's physical size, then immediately corrects a potential misunderstanding by emphasizing its great knowledge.
The key is to see the contrast between 'small in size' and 'great in knowledge'.
2
Identify the correction
Find where the commentator addresses the possible misconception that small size equals little knowledge. Understand why he makes this clarification.
This correction is central to the lesson.
3
List the contents
Identify the specific theological categories and proofs mentioned: necessary, impossible, permissible for Allah and His messengers, and the definitive proofs that lead to certainty.
These are the building blocks of Islamic theology (kalam).
4
Understand the purpose
Reflect on why these contents are important: they free a believer from blind imitation (taqlid) and bring them to the light of verification (tahqiq), ensuring no doubt in faith.
This shows the practical goal of the poem.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الأوّل فقوله: (صغيرة في الحَجم) أي : القدر، وصف كاشف أبياتُها أحد وسبعون بيتاً ولمَّا كان هذا الوصف يوهم أنَّها قليلة العلم استدرك عليه بأن رفع هذا التوهم بقوله : (لكنَّها كبيرة) أي : عظيمة (في العلم) أي: المعاني المدلولة لها؛ وذلك لأنَّها اشتملت على بيان ما يجب الله تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وعلى مثل ذلك في حقِّ رُسُله عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام، وعلى البراهين القطعية التي يخرج بها المكلف من ربقة التقليد إلى نور التحقيق، حتى لا يكون في إيمانه خلاف، وسيأتي بيان الخلاف في إيمان المقلّد إن شاء الله تعالى وعلى الرَّدِّ على أهل الضَّلال.
And according to the first (interpretation), his saying: '(Small) in size' means: in magnitude, a description, its lines number seventy-one. Commentary
This passage is an explanatory argument that exalts the poem despite its small size. The commentator first notes that the description 'small in size' refers to its length of 71 lines. He then points out a potential misunderstanding: that small size implies little knowledge. To correct this, the author adds 'but great in knowledge,' meaning the meanings contained. The commentator then explains why the poem is great in knowledge: it includes the necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes of God and likewise of His prophets, as well as definitive proofs that free the believer from blind imitation (taqlid) to verification (tahqiq), ensuring certainty in faith. It also mentions future discussion on the faith of the imitator and the refutation of heretics. The argument follows the order: statement of size, correction of misunderstanding, justification by content.

Correction: The poem is actually great in knowledge because it contains the core of Islamic theology and definitive proofs that free from blind imitation.

The Ten Principles of Kalam
Al-Sawi enumerates the ten principles of kalam with brief explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten foundational principles of the science of kalam as enumerated by al-Sawi, including their definitions and significance.

Core Idea
Every science rests on ten principles; kalam's principles define its identity and purpose.

Before diving into theological arguments, it is essential to know the framework of the discipline itself. Al-Sawi lists the ten principles that underpin the science of kalam, explaining its names, definition, subject, aim, founder, ruling, relation, issues, derivation, and benefit. This passage provides the complete map of kalam scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the enumeration style: first the names, then the four principles from the sharh, then the remaining six.
2
Identify the ten principles
The passage lists ten principles. See if you can list them in order as they appear. The first four are mentioned by the sharh, the rest are given with brief explanations.
3
Understand the four principles from the sharh
The explainer (al-sharih) mentions four: the name, definition, subject matter, and aim. Note that the passage only elaborates on the name; the other three are not expanded here.
4
Explore the remaining six principles
For each of the remaining six—founder, ruling, relation, issues, derivation, benefit—read the brief explanation given. Consider why each is important for defining a science.
5
Reflect on the purpose of this enumeration
Ask yourself why these ten principles are considered necessary for anyone entering the study of kalam. How do they help structure the discipline?
This connects to the broader goal of understanding kalam's foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبادئ العشرة
ويسمَّى علم التَّوحيد وعلم أصول الدين وعلم العقائد.
مطلب في المبادئ العشرة
قوله : (ويسمى : علم التَّوحيد) أي ويسمّى أيضاً علم الكلام، ووجه تسميته بهذه الأسماء ظاهر، وهو أحد المبادئ العشرة التي لا بدَّ لكلِّ شارع في فن من معرفتها، وإلا كان شروعه عبثاً. ذكر الشارح منها أربعة : وهي الاسم والحد، والموضوع، والغاية، وبقي واضعه وحكمه ونسبته ومسائله واستمداده، وفائدته. فواضعه: الأشاعرة والماتريديَّة؛ أي: الَّذين دوّنوا كتبه وردُّوا على فرق الضَّلال، وإلَّا فالتَّوحيد جاء به كلّ نبي من آدم إلى سيدنا محمد. وحكمه: الوجوب العيني على كل مكلف بالدليل ولو إجمالياً، والكفائي بخصوص التفصيلي. ونسبته : أنَّه أصل العلوم الدينية وما سواه فرع. ومسائله : الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات. واستمداده من الكتاب والسُّنَّة والعقل. وفائدته : معرفة العقائد الصّحيحة والفاسدة.
The ten principles. And it is called the science of tawhid (tawḥīd), the science of the principles of religion (uṣūl al-dīn), and the science of creeds (al-ʿaqāʾid). A section on the ten principles. His saying: (and it is called the science of tawhid) i.e. it is also called the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām), and the reason for its naming with these names is apparent. And it is one of the ten principles which every entrant into an art must know, otherwise his entry would be futile. The explainer (al-shāriḥ) mentioned four of them: the name (al-ism), the definition (al-ḥadd), the subject matter (al-mawḍūʿ), and the aim (al-ghāya). And there remain: its founder (wāḍiʿuhu), its ruling (ḥukmuhu), its relation (nisbatuhu), its issues (masāʾiluhu), its derivation (istimdādahu), and its benefit (fāʾidatuhu). Its founder: the Ashʿarīs and the Māturīdīs – meaning those who compiled its books and refuted the sects of misguidance; otherwise, tawhid was brought by every prophet from Adam to our master Muhammad. Its ruling: individual obligation (wujūb ʿaynī) upon every legally responsible person (mukallaf) through evidence, even if general (ijmālī), and collective obligation (kifāʾī) specifically with regard to detailed evidence. Its relation: that it is the root of the religious sciences and whatever is other than it is a branch. Its issues: the necessary (al-wājibāt), the impossible (al-mustaḥīlāt), and the possible (al-jāʾizāt). Its derivation: from the Book, the Sunnah, and the intellect. Its benefit: knowledge of correct and corrupt creeds.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the ten principles (al-mabādiʾ al-ʿashara) that are foundational to any discipline, specifically applied to the science of kalam. The text first states that this science is known by several names: the science of tawhid (monotheism), the science of the principles of religion (uṣūl al-dīn), and the science of creeds (al-ʿaqāʾid). The commentator then explains that the author mentioned four of these ten principles from the matn: the name (i.e., the various names of the discipline), the definition (its technical definition), the subject matter (what it studies), and the aim (its purpose). Then he lists the remaining six principles: (1) its founder—the Ashʿarī and Māturīdī schools, as they compiled works and defended orthodoxy, though the content of tawhid was taught by all prophets; (2) its ruling—it is an individual obligation (farḍ ʿayn) to know the fundamentals with general proofs, and a collective obligation (farḍ kifāya) to master detailed proofs; (3) its relation—it is the root of all religious sciences, and other sciences are branches; (4) its issues—the three categories of judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible with respect to God; (5) its derivation—its evidence comes from the Qur'an, Sunnah, and reason; (6) its benefit—distinguishing correct beliefs from false ones. The passage thus provides a complete overview of the discipline's essential characteristics.

علم التوحيد
علم الكلام
أصول الدين
العقائد
الاسم
الحد
الموضوع
الغاية
الواضع
الحكم
النسبة
المسائل
الاستمداد
الفائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The founder of kalam is a single person or a specific earlier scholar.
Correction: The founder refers to the schools of Ashʿarīs and Māturīdīs who systematized the discipline; the core truths of tawhid were taught by all prophets.

Checkpoint

A) The name, the definition, the subject matter, and the founder
B) The name, the definition, the subject matter, and the aim
C) The name, the ruling, the issues, and the benefit
D) The founder, the ruling, the relation, and the derivation

Key Takeaway
The science of kalam is defined by ten principles that outline its name, definition, subject, aim, founder, ruling, relation, issues, derivation, and benefit, establishing it as the foundational discipline of Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Definition of the Science of Tawhid
Al-Sawi defines 'ilm al-tawhid and explains its key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'ilm al-tawhid and the meaning of each component of the definition.

Core Idea
Tawhid is a systematic discipline using certain proofs from reason and revelation.

Al-Sawi begins by providing the precise definition of the science of tawhid. He then explains each key term, showing that this is not mere speculation but a rigorous science grounded in certain evidence. This passage lays the foundation for understanding the nature and method of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and commentary
Read the Arabic definition once, then read the English translation. Notice how the commentary unpacks each phrase.
The definition is dense; the commentary clarifies each term.
2
Identify the four key phrases
The definition has four main components: 'science', 'enabled', 'religious beliefs', 'derived from certain evidences'. Understand what each adds.
These components form the core of the definition.
3
Understand what 'science' means here
The commentary explains that 'science' in this context refers to the rules and principles of the discipline, not the faculty of knowledge or mere perception.
This distinguishes tawhid from simple belief.
4
Understand 'enabled' and 'religious'
The science provides the ability (yuqtadar) to establish beliefs, and 'religious' means attributed to the true religion (al-dīn al-ḥaqq).
This shows the practical purpose and scope.
5
Understand 'certain evidences'
The beliefs are derived from certain evidences, which the commentator clarifies are both rational (ʿaqliyya) and transmitted (naqliyya, i.e., scriptural).
This establishes the sources of theological proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف علم التوحيد : وهو: علم يُقتدر به على إثبات العقائد الدينية المكتسبة من أدلتها اليقينية . قوله : (وهو علم ) : أي : وحده علم ... إلخ، والمراد بالعلم هنا : القواعد والضَّوابط لا الملكة ولا الإدراك. قوله : (يقتدر ) : أي : يتقوى به . قوله : (الدينيَّة) : أي : المنسوبة للدين الحقِّ . وقوله: (المكتسبة من أدلتها ... إلخ) : أي : الَّتي أنتجتها الأدلّة اليقينيَّة، واليقينيَّة منسوبة لليقين والمراد: الأدلة العقليّة والنَّقليَّة.
The definition of the science of tawhid: It is a science by which one is enabled to establish religious beliefs (al-ʿaqāʾid al-dīniyya) derived from their certain evidences (adillatuhā al-yaqīniyya). His saying: (and it is a science) i.e., it alone is a science, etc., and what is meant by 'science' here is the rules and principles (al-qawāʿid wa al-ḍawābiṭ), not the faculty (al-malaka) nor perception (al-idrāk). His saying: (enabled) i.e., one is strengthened by it. His saying: (religious) i.e., attributed to the true religion. And his saying: (derived from their evidences, etc.) i.e., those which the certain evidences have produced, and 'certain' (yaqīniyya) is attributed to certainty (al-yaqīn), and what is meant is rational and transmitted evidences (al-adilla al-ʿaqliyya wa al-naqliyya).
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of the science of tawhid (ʿilm al-tawḥīd) and explains each component of the definition. The definition states: 'It is a science by which one is enabled to establish religious beliefs derived from their certain evidences.' The commentator then unpacks key terms: first, 'science' (ʿilm) here does not mean the cognitive faculty or mere perception; rather, it refers to the set of rules and principles (qawāʿid wa ḍawābiṭ) that constitute the discipline. Second, 'enabled' (yuqtadar) means 'one is strengthened by it'—the science provides the ability to prove beliefs. Third, 'religious' (dīniyya) specifies beliefs that belong to the true religion (al-dīn al-ḥaqq). Fourth, 'derived from their certain evidences' (al-muktasaba min adillatihā al-yaqīniyya) means that these beliefs are produced by certain (yaqīnī) evidences, which the commentator clarifies are of two kinds: rational (ʿaqliyya) and transmitted (naqliyya, i.e., scriptural). The definition thus emphasizes that tawhid is a systematic discipline (not mere faith) that uses apodictic proofs from reason and revelation to establish correct doctrines.

Key Terms
علم التوحيد
العقائد الدينية
الأدلة اليقينية
الملكة
الإدراك
القواعد والضوابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'science' (ʿilm) in the definition refers to empirical knowledge or the cognitive faculty in general.
Correction: The text explicitly states that 'science' here means the rules and principles (al-qawāʿid wa al-ḍawābiṭ) that constitute the discipline, not the faculty (al-malaka) nor perception (al-idrāk).

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is intended by 'science' (علم) in the definition of tawhid?
A) The cognitive faculty
B) Perception
C) The rules and principles
D) Certainty

Key Takeaway
The science of tawhid is a systematic discipline defined by its goal of establishing religious beliefs through certain rational and scriptural proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the science of tawhid is built on certain proofs from both reason and revelation. Have you ever personally examined the rational evidence for the existence of Allah and His attributes, or do you rely solely on blind imitation? What specific belief are you least certain about, and what would it take for you to establish it with certain proof?
?
The definition emphasizes that tawhid gives you the 'ability' to establish your beliefs. If your faith were tested by a serious doubt today, would you have the tools to defend it with certainty? What step will you take this week to acquire that ability — to actually learn a rational proof or memorize a scriptural evidence?
?
The passage warns that 'science' here is not mere knowledge but a system of principles. Are your Islamic beliefs organized by sound principles, or are they just scattered ideas you picked up? Will you commit to studying this science systematically, not just accumulating facts, but learning how to derive and defend your beliefs with evidence?

Definition of Ilm al-Tawhid and Its Parts
Al-Sawi defines Ilm al-Tawhid and explains each component of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sawi defines the science of Tawhid and what each part of the definition means.

Core Idea
Tawhid is a systematic science grounded in certain proofs, both rational and transmitted.

Al-Sawi begins by giving a precise definition of Ilm al-Tawhid. This definition is not just a statement; it is carefully worded to distinguish this science from mere intuition or opinion. The subsequent commentary unpacks each term, showing that this science empowers one to prove religious beliefs using certain evidence. Let's read the passage and see how each phrase contributes to a rigorous understanding of Tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and commentary
First, read the Arabic definition and its literal translation. Then read the commentary that explains each phrase.
Pay attention to how the commentary clarifies the meaning of each term.
2
Understand 'science' as rules, not faculty
The commentary specifies that 'علم' here means a set of rules and principles, not the faculty of knowledge or perception.
This distinguishes Tawhid from intuitive knowledge.
3
Note the empowerment aspect
The phrase 'يقتدر' means one is enabled or strengthened by this science to establish beliefs.
This shows Tawhid is a practical tool for proof.
4
Identify the source of beliefs
Beliefs are 'acquired from certain proofs' which are both rational (عقلية) and transmitted (نقلية).
This grounds Tawhid in both reason and revelation.
5
Summarize the definition in your own words
Try to rephrase the definition and its parts as a complete sentence without looking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف علم التوحيد :

Definition of the science of Tawhid: It is a science by which one is enabled to establish religious beliefs acquired from its certain proofs. His statement: (religious) means: attributed to the true religion. And His statement: (acquired from its proofs... etc.) means: those which have been produced by certain proofs, and 'certain' is attributed to certainty, and what is meant is the intellectual and transmitted proofs.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage defines Ilm al-Tawhid (the science of divine unity) and explains its definition phrase by phrase. The definition states that it is a science (علم) that gives one the ability to affirm religious beliefs (العقائد الدينية) that are derived from its certain proofs. The commentary first clarifies that 'science' here refers to a body of rules and principles (القواعد والضوابط), not the faculty of knowing or the act of perception. Then it explains 'يقتدر' as 'he is enabled' or 'strengthened,' indicating that this science empowers one to prove beliefs. 'الدينية' is explained as 'attributed to the true religion' (الدين الحق). Finally, 'المكتسبة من أدلتها' is explained as beliefs produced by certain proofs (الأدلة اليقينية), and those certain proofs are specified as both intellectual (عقلية) and transmitted (نقلية) proofs. Thus the definition establishes that tawhid is a rigorous science based on certain evidence, both rational and revealed.

The Subject Matter of Tawhid: Three Scholarly Opinions
Al-Sawi enumerates three scholarly opinions on the subject matter of Ilm al-Tawhid and declares the third as most preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main scholarly opinions regarding the subject matter of Ilm al-Tawhid and why the comprehensive third opinion is considered most preponderant.

Core Idea
The subject of Tawhid is comprehensive: Allah's essence, possible beings, and all intelligible matters.

Al-Sawi begins by establishing what Ilm al-Tawhid studies. He presents three views: the first focuses on Allah's essence and attributes, the second on possible beings as evidence for God, and the third expands to include all intelligible matters—necessary, possible, impossible, and prophetic news. Understanding this debate clarifies the scope of tawhid and prepares you for deeper theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses technical terms like 'عوارض ذاتية'.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage presents three opinions on the subject matter. Find each one and notice how they are introduced: first with 'وموضوعه', then 'وقيل', then 'وقيل'. Look for the reasoning behind each.
The third opinion is introduced as 'غير ذلك' and is described as more preponderant.
3
Analyze why the third opinion is preferred
The author states the third opinion is أرجح (more preponderant). Read the reasons given: it includes the three categories (necessary, possible, impossible), existents and non-existents, and matters related to prophets and the afterlife. Reflect on why comprehensiveness matters in defining a science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وموضوعه : ذات الإله : أي موضوع هذا العلم ذات الإله من : حيث إثبات الصفات الكماليَّة والتنزيهيَّة ؛ بأن تجعل ذات الإله موضوعاً تُحمل عليه الصفات، بحيث تقول : ذات الإله يجب لها الوجود والقدم والقدرة . . . إلى آخرها فيكون المراد بالموضوع المصطلح عليه عند المناطقة، المعبّر عنه بالمسند إليه عند البيانيين وبالمبتدأ عند النحويين، فموضوع كلِّ فنّ ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذَّاتيَّة، وإن كان التَّعبير بالعوارض في هذا الفنّ تسمحاً، إذ المراد منها هنا صفاته تعالى، ويستحيل وصفها بالعوارض، إذ هي من سمات الحوادث، وهي مستحيلة على ذاته تعالى وعلى صفاته .
المبادئ العشرة
وقيل : الممكنات، وقيل : غير ذلك .
وقولنا : (عوارضه) أي: الأمور التي تعرض له، وتطرأ عليه؛ كالتَّعجب والفرح والحزن وغيرها مما يعرض للإنسان.
وقولنا : (الذَّاتِيَّة) نسبة للذَّات، ومعنى كونها ذاتيَّة : أنَّها لازمة للذات بالفعل أو بالقوة، أو لا تنفكُ عنها، فخرج غير الذاتية؛ كحركة الأبيض بواسطة كونه حيواناً ، وذلك أنَّ كونه حيواناً عن حقيقته .
قوله: (وقيل الممكنات أي قيل : إنَّ موضوع هذا العلم الممكنات من حيث دلالتها على موجدها واتصافه بالصِّفات الكماليَّة والتَّنزيهيَّة، وبيان كون الممكنات موضوعاً أن تقول : الممكنات حادثة، وكلُّ حادث له محدث، ثمَّ هذا المحدث لا بد أن يكون موجوداً قديماً ... إلى آخر الصفات .
قوله : (وقيل : غير ذلك) : المراد بهذا الغير المعلومات موجودة أو معدومة فيشمل الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، بحيث تقول : الصفات الواجبة ثابتة الله ، وتقول في الجائزات : الممكنات حادثة، وكلُّ حادث لا بدَّ له من محدث، ثمَّ تنقل الكلام إلى المحدث من حيث وجوده وقدمه . . . إلخ، وتقول في المستحيلات : النَّقص مستحيل عليه تعالى وهكذا .
وهذا القول الثَّالث أرجح ؛ لأنَّه يشمل الأقسام الثلاثة، ويشمل الموجودات والمعدومات وما يتعلَّق بالرُّسل من واجب وجائز ومستحيل، ويشمل أيضاً المسموعات من البعث والنشر والحشر، وغير ذلك من كلّ ما أخبر به الصَّادق المصدوق، كذا قرَّره مؤلّفه .
His statement: (And its subject matter is the Essence of God): meaning the subject matter of this science is the Essence of God in terms of establishing the attributes of perfection and transcendence, such that the Essence of God is made the subject about which the attributes are predicated, so you say: the Essence of God necessarily has existence, eternality, power... etc., so what is meant is the subject as defined by logicians, expressed as the predicate in bayan and the subject in grammar. For the subject matter of every art is that about which its essential accidents are investigated, although the expression 'accidents' in this art is a license, for what is meant here are His attributes, and it is impossible to describe them as accidents, since they are characteristics of contingents, and they are impossible for His Essence and His attributes. The ten principles. And it is said: possible things, and it is said: other than that. And our statement: (its accidents) means the matters that happen to it and occur upon it, like astonishment, joy, sadness, and other things that happen to a human. And our statement: (essential) is relative to the essence, and the meaning of their being essential is that they are inseparable from the essence actually or potentially, or they do not separate from it, so non-essential is excluded, like the movement of a white thing due to its being an animal, and that is because its being an animal is part of its reality. His statement: (And it is said: possible things) means it is said: the subject matter of this science is possible things in terms of their indication of their Originator and His being qualified with attributes of perfection and transcendence, and the explanation of possible things being the subject is that you say: possible things are originated, and every originated thing has an originator, then this originator must be eternal... etc. His statement: (And it is said: other than that) means by this 'other' is intended information, existing or non-existing, so it includes necessary, possible, and impossible things, such that you say: the necessary attributes are established for God, and you say about possible things: possible things are originated, and every originated thing necessarily has an originator, then you transfer the discussion to the originator regarding his existence and eternity... etc., and you say about impossible things: deficiency is impossible for Him, and so forth. And this third statement is more preponderant because it includes the three categories, and includes existents and non-existents and what is related to the messengers of necessary, possible, and impossible, and also includes the heard matters of resurrection, gathering, and reckoning, and other than that from everything about which the truthful and believed one informed us. Thus its author decided it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses the subject matter (موضوع) of Ilm al-Tawhid. It begins by presenting the first opinion: that the subject is the Essence of God (ذات الإله) in terms of establishing His attributes of perfection (الكمالية) and transcendence (التنزيهية). The text explains that this follows the logicians' definition of a subject as that about which essential accidents are predicated, though using 'accidents' here is a license since God's attributes cannot be called accidents. Then, after a brief interpolation mentioning 'المبادئ العشرة' (the ten principles, possibly a section heading), the second opinion is introduced: some say the subject is possible things (الممكنات) insofar as they indicate their Creator and His attributes. The explanation shows how possible things are used as evidence for God's existence and attributes. The third opinion is then presented: the subject is 'other than that' (غير ذلك), meaning all information (المعلومات) whether existing or non-existing, thus including necessary beings (واجبات), possible beings (جائزات), and impossible beings (مستحيلات). Examples are given for each category. Finally, the author judges the third opinion as more preponderant (أرجح) because it encompasses all three categories, includes existents and non-existents, matters related to prophets, and the heard matters of the afterlife, covering everything that the truthful and believed (the Prophet) informed about. Thus the subject matter is comprehensive.

العوارض الذاتية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the subject matter of Tawhid is limited to God's essence or only to possible beings.
Correction: The third opinion shows it includes all necessary, possible, and impossible matters, as well as information about prophets and the afterlife.

Checkpoint

A) It restricts the subject to possible things only

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of 'knowing Allah,' do you restrict it to memorizing His attributes, or does it extend to how you see every possible thing in your life as pointing back to Him? Where do you fall short in making that connection daily?
?
The third opinion includes matters like resurrection and judgment. Are you living today as if those 'masmu'at' are as concrete as the reality you can see? What would change if you truly believed that the gathering and reckoning are part of the subject matter of your faith?
?
This lesson shows that the subject of Tawhid is comprehensive, covering necessary, possible, and impossible. But does your heart feel the weight of the impossible—that deficiency is impossible for Allah? How does that affect your trust in Him when you face your own deficiencies?

The Comprehensive Subject of Kalam
Al-Sawi presents and preponderates the third opinion on the subject of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preponderant opinion on the subject of kalam includes necessary, possible, impossible, and revealed matters, and how this comprehensiveness is justified.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is not limited to possible things; it includes all rational categories and revealed truths.

In this passage, Al-Sawi presents the third and most comprehensive opinion on the subject (mawḍūʿ) of kalām. While earlier views limited it to possible things or to all knowable things in general, this opinion explicitly includes necessary, possible, impossible, existents, non-existents, and the heard matters (masmūʿāt) from revelation. The commentary explains why this view is preponderant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the flow from one opinion to the next.
The passage is a commentary on a base text, so it explains each opinion in turn.
2
Identify the three opinions presented
The passage discusses three sayings (qīl): first, that the subject is possible things; second, that it is 'other than that' meaning all known existents and non-existents; third, the same as the second but declared more preponderant.
The third saying is actually the second opinion, but it is called 'third' as a distinct position.
3
Understand the preponderance argument
Ask yourself why the author says the third opinion is more preponderant. The passage gives the reason: it encompasses the three categories (necessary, possible, impossible), existents and non-existents, prophetic attributes, and revealed matters like resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقيل الممكنات أي قيل : إنَّ موضوع هذا العلم الممكنات من حيث دلالتها على موجدها واتصافه بالصِّفات الكماليَّة والتَّنزيهيَّة، وبيان كون الممكنات موضوعاً أن تقول : الممكنات حادثة، وكلُّ حادث له محدث، ثمَّ هذا المحدث لا بد أن يكون موجوداً قديماً ... إلى آخر الصفات . قوله : (وقيل : غير ذلك) : المراد بهذا الغير المعلومات موجودة أو معدومة فيشمل الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، بحيث تقول : الصفات الواجبة ثابتة الله ، وتقول في الجائزات : الممكنات حادثة، وكلُّ حادث لا بدَّ له من محدث، ثمَّ تنقل الكلام إلى المحدث من حيث وجوده وقدمه . . . إلخ، وتقول في المستحيلات : النَّقص مستحيل عليه تعالى وهكذا . وهذا القول الثَّالث أرجح ؛ لأنَّه يشمل الأقسام الثلاثة، ويشمل الموجودات والمعدومات وما يتعلَّق بالرُّسل من واجب وجائز ومستحيل، ويشمل أيضاً المسموعات من البعث والنشر والحشر، وغير ذلك من كلّ ما أخبر به الصَّادق المصدوق، كذا قرَّره مؤلّفه .
His statement: 'And it was said: the possible things' means: it was said that the subject of this science is the possible things, in terms of their indication of their Originator and His being described with the attributes of perfection and transcendence, and the statement that possible things are subject is that you say: possible things are originated, and every originated thing has an originator, then this originator must be eternal ... to the end of the attributes. His statement: 'And it was said: other than that' means by this 'other' the known existent or non-existent, so it includes the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, such that you say: the necessary attributes are established for God, and you say regarding the possible: possible things are originated, and every originated thing must have an originator, then you transfer the discourse to the originator in terms of His existence and pre-eternity ... etc., and you say regarding the impossible: deficiency is impossible for Him, exalted. And so on. And this third saying is more preponderant because it encompasses the three categories, and it encompasses existents and non-existents and what relates to the messengers of necessary, possible, and impossible, and it also encompasses the heard matters of resurrection, spreading, gathering, and other than that of everything informed by the truthful and believed. Thus its author decided.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents and preponderates a third opinion on the subject (mawḍūʿ) of the science of kalām. First, it explains two earlier views: one that limits the subject to possible things (al-mumkināt) in their aspect of indicating the Creator and His attributes; another that expands it to all knowable things—necessary, possible, and impossible—so that the discourse covers the necessary attributes, the origination of possible things, and the impossibility of deficiency in God. The author then declares the third opinion—which is actually the second one described, but labelled 'third' because it is formulated as a distinct position—to be more preponderant (arjaḥ). The reason is that it includes all three rational categories (necessary, possible, impossible), existents and non-existents, what pertains to prophets (obligatory, possible, impossible for them), and also the 'heard matters' (al-masmūʿāt) such as resurrection, spreading, and gathering—everything reported by the truthful and believed. Thus the subject is maximally comprehensive, covering both rational and revealed topics.

Key Terms
موضوع علم الكلام
الممكنات
الواجبات
الجائزات
المستحيلات
المسموعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the subject of kalam is only possible things.
Correction: This passage shows it also includes necessary, impossible, and revealed matters.

Key Takeaway

Kalam aims for both worldly knowledge and otherworldly bliss.
